Written by Jotika Teli—Certified Public Accountant with 24 years of experience. Read Jotika's full bio Published Thursday 22 January 2026 Table of contents What is marginal cost? What is the marginal cost formula? How to calculate marginal cost What are the main components of marginal cost? Why is marginal cost important for business planning? Marginal revenue vs marginal cost Ways to apply marginal cost to production decisions Why you should calculate your marginal cost accurately Track your costs and optimize decisions with Xero FAQs on marginal cost formula Key takeaways Calculate marginal cost using the formula "Change in Total Cost ÷ Change in Quantity" to determine the exact expense of producing one additional unit and make informed production decisions. Compare marginal cost to your selling price before increasing production. Only expand output when the selling price exceeds marginal cost to ensure each additional unit generates profit. Apply the profit optimization rule by producing more units when marginal revenue exceeds marginal cost, and reduce production when marginal cost exceeds marginal revenue to maximize profitability. Track both variable costs (materials, hourly labor, utilities) and fixed costs (equipment, rent, salaries) separately when calculating marginal cost, since only variable costs typically change with additional production. What is marginal cost? Marginal cost is the additional cost of producing one extra unit of your product or service. It helps you make critical production and pricing decisions that maximize profits. Understanding marginal cost matters because it helps you: Determine optimal production levels: Know when to increase or decrease output Set competitive prices: Price products to maximize revenue without losing customers Plan business expansion: Make informed decisions about scaling operations Manage cash flow: Understand the financial impact of production changes What is the marginal cost formula? The marginal cost formula calculates the cost of producing one additional unit: Marginal Cost = Change in Total Cost ÷ Change in Quantity Use this formula to pinpoint how changes in production volume will affect your costs and, ultimately, your profitability. How to calculate marginal cost Calculating marginal cost involves a few simple steps. Following this process helps ensure your numbers are accurate, giving you a clear picture of your production expenses. Understanding change in costs The 'change in costs' is the difference between the total cost of production at two different output levels. This includes any additional variable costs, like materials or hourly labor, needed to produce more units. Understanding change in quantity The 'change in quantity' is the difference in the number of units produced. For most marginal cost calculations, you'll look at the impact of producing just one more unit, so the change in quantity is often one. Step-by-step calculation process Step-by-step calculation process: Calculate current total costs: Add all production expenses for your current output level Calculate new total costs: Add all production expenses if you produce one more unit Find the change in total cost: Subtract current costs from new total costs Divide by quantity change: Since you're calculating for one additional unit, divide by 1 Let's say Mohammed wants to expand his bakery. He currently makes 100 cakes and wants to make one more. His usual production costs are 1000 (10 per cake); these costs increase to $1005 if he makes that additional cake. First, he works out the change in total cost: $1005 – $1000 = $5 Second, he works out the marginal cost: $5 / 1.0 = $5 The marginal cost for Mohammed to produce one more cake is $5. Since this is lower than his average cost of $10 per cake, expanding production is profitable. Using marginal cost for business decisions: When marginal cost < average cost: Increase production to reduce overall costs per unit and boost profits When marginal cost > average cost: Maintain current production levels to avoid increasing overall costs When marginal cost = selling price: You've reached the break-even point for additional units What are the main components of marginal cost? Marginal cost components include all production-related expenses that change when you produce additional units. Variable costs increase with production volume: Materials: Raw materials, supplies, and inventory Labor: Hourly wages and overtime pay Utilities: Energy, heating, and operational expenses Fixed costs remain constant regardless of production: Equipment: Machinery, tools, and technology Facilities: Rent, insurance, and property costs Salaries: Management and administrative staff wages Why is marginal cost important for business planning? Marginal cost analysis guides critical business decisions by showing you the true cost of growth and optimal production levels. Key business decisions marginal cost helps you make: Production planning: Determine how many units to produce for maximum profit Pricing strategy: Set prices that cover costs while remaining competitive Growth timing: Know when expansion will increase or decrease profitability Resource allocation: Decide where to invest time and money for best returns Marginal revenue vs marginal cost Marginal revenue is the additional income earned from selling one extra unit. Compare it to marginal cost to maximize profits. Marginal Revenue = Change in Total Revenue ÷ Change in Quantity Profit optimization rule: When marginal revenue > marginal cost: Increase production to boost profits When marginal revenue < marginal cost: Decrease production to avoid losses When marginal revenue = marginal cost: You've reached optimal production levels Example of marginal revenue Let's say Alison sells wallets at a market stall for $30 each. If she can't sell all her stock, she could supply the surplus walletsto another vendor across town at a rate of $20. But is this worth it? How many wallets does she need to sell to increase her profits or just to break even? From the image above, we see her marginal revenue would be $20 for the sale of one extra wallet. If her marginal cost is higher than this – say, $22 – then she would not make a profit on this single-unit transaction. From here, she must work out how to make her marginal cost equal her marginal revenue. She has a few options: Perhaps she can negotiate a higher unit price for a larger wholesale order? By selling more wallets she would decrease their cost per unit. She might sell to a vendor nearer to where she is and is therefore cheaper to get to. She might find a less expensive way to travel. Alison can compare what she earns from each option with what it costs her, so she can see which choice makes her more profit. Ways to apply marginal cost to production decisions Applying marginal cost analysis helps you make smarter, more profitable production decisions. It provides a clear framework for deciding whether to scale up, scale down, or maintain your current output. Here's how you can use it: Identify profitable opportunities: If the price you can sell a product for is higher than the marginal cost to produce it, increasing production is likely a good move. Avoid unnecessary losses: If the marginal cost to produce one more item is higher than its selling price, you'll lose money on that unit. This signals that you should avoid increasing production. Find your optimal volume: The ideal production level is often where marginal cost equals marginal revenue. At this point, you're maximizing your profit on production. Why you should calculate your marginal cost accurately Accurate marginal cost calculations drive three key business improvements: Optimize pricing decisions: Set prices that maximize revenue while remaining competitive in your market Improve production planning: Determine whether additional units will increase or decrease overall profitability Allocate resources efficiently: Decide which products to prioritize and when to invest in equipment or facilities For more guidance on managing your business, you can refer to government publications like the US Government Accountability Office (GAO) Cost Estimating and Assessment Guide, which outlines best practices for developing and managing program costs. Track your costs and optimize decisions with Xero Making smart business decisions starts with having clear financial data. Xero accounting software gives you real-time insights into your costs, revenue, and profitability, so you can apply concepts like marginal cost with confidence. Track expenses, manage inventory, and run reports to see where your business stands. Ready to take control of your numbers? Get one month free and see how Xero makes financial management easy. FAQs on marginal cost formula Here are answers to some common questions about marginal cost. What's the difference between marginal cost and average cost? Marginal cost is the expense of producing one additional unit, while average cost is the total cost of production divided by the total number of units produced. Marginal cost tells you about the cost of the next unit, while average cost gives you a picture of the overall cost per unit. When should I use marginal cost analysis for my business? Use marginal cost analysis when you're making decisions about production volume. It's especially helpful when you're considering whether to increase output to meet demand, take on a new order, or change your pricing strategy. What are common mistakes when calculating marginal cost? Common causes of increasing marginal costs: Labor expenses: Overtime pay, hiring additional workers, or increased hourly rates Material costs: Higher supplier prices or sourcing from more expensive vendors Capacity constraints: Need for new equipment, facilities, or infrastructure to increase production Inefficiencies: Production bottlenecks or quality control issues that increase per-unit costs How does marginal cost help with pricing decisions? Marginal cost directly impacts supply and demand decisions by determining profitable production levels. Production decision rules: When selling price > marginal cost: Increase production to capture additional profit When selling price < marginal cost: Reduce production to avoid losses on each additional unit When selling price = marginal cost: Maintain current production levels at break-even point Disclaimer Xero does not provide accounting, tax, business or legal advice. This guide has been provided for information purposes only. You should consult your own professional advisors for advice directly relating to your business or before taking action in relation to any of the content provided. OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring: Essential TCO Guide 2026 Solutions Back Energy Monitoring Indoor Air Quality Temperature Monitoring Water Leak Detection Monitoring as a Service ENERGY STAR Certification Industries Back Commercial Real Estate Data Centers Food Processing Healthcare Hotels Manufacturing Offices Restaurants Retail Schools & Colleges Senior Living Warehouses Resources Back Blog Building Performance Guides City Guides Compliance Guides ENERGY STAR Guides How-To Guides Regulatory Guides Tel 888 812 1971 Tel 888 812 1971 OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring: Essential TCO Analysis Guide 2026
The facility manager stared at a $275,000 BMS proposal, wondering if there was a better way to achieve building intelligence without depleting the entire year's capital budget. With traditional building management systems requiring substantial upfront investments often exceeding $250,000 for mid-sized facilities, the emergence of subscription-based monitoring solutions has fundamentally changed how organizations approach building intelligence. Understanding the true total cost of ownership across both OpEx vs CapEx monitoring models is essential for making an informed investment decision. Commercial buildings account for approximately 40% of U.S. energy consumption, with the U.S. Department of Energy reporting that 30% of this energy is wasted due to inefficiencies. This waste translates to billions of dollars in unnecessary operating costs annually. The OpEx vs CapEx monitoring framework determines not only how you pay for building intelligence but also how quickly you can capture these savings and adapt to evolving technology. Whether you manage a single facility or an entire portfolio across healthcare facilities, manufacturing plants, or commercial real estate, the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring analysis requires examining far more than purchase price versus subscription fees. This comprehensive guide breaks down the hidden costs, timeline implications, and long-term financial impact of each approach to help you make the right choice for your organization's specific situation and goals. Compare capital investment versus subscription models to make the right monitoring choice for your facility. 30% Energy Wasted in Commercial Buildings 90% Buildings Without Smart Functionality 10 Days Average IoT Implementation Timeline Get Your Free Assessment Call (888) 812-1971 OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring: Head-to-Head Comparison Understanding the core differences between capital expenditure and operating expenditure monitoring models is essential before making infrastructure decisions. The OpEx vs CapEx monitoring comparison spans cost structure, deployment timeline, technical requirements, and long-term flexibility. Each approach serves different organizational needs and building types, and the right choice depends on your specific operational requirements, budget constraints, and long-term facility plans. Traditional building management systems have dominated commercial facilities for decades, offering comprehensive control over HVAC, lighting, and security systems through centralized automation requiring significant capital investment. Modern IoT monitoring platforms have emerged as OpEx alternatives that prioritize visibility, analytics, and rapid deployment over direct equipment control. The following comparison uses verified cost data from the National Renewable Energy Laboratory and industry research to help you make an informed decision. Traditional BMS (CapEx Model) Upfront Cost: 5.20-14.18 per sq ft 1 100K Sq Ft Example: 520,000-1,418,000 Labor Portion: 50-75% of total cost 1 Deployment Time: 6-18 months typical ROI Timeline: ~4 years payback 2 Control Capability: Direct automated equipment control 5-Year TCO: $776,000 – 1,418,000+ 1
IoT Monitoring (OpEx Model)
Upfront Cost: ~0.75 per sq ft 2 100K Sq Ft Example: ~$75,000 Monthly Subscription: 750-1,500 3 Deployment Time: Days to weeks ROI Timeline: ~2 years payback 2 Control Capability: Visibility and analytics focus 5-Year TCO: $45,000 – $90,000 3 1 NREL/JISEA 2022 Commercial Building Sensors and Controls Systems Study – Cost data from project invoices, RSMeans, and GSA schedules. 2 CREtech/CANDI Controls Analysis – IoT systems at $0.75/sq ft vs $2.50+ for traditional BMS; BMS 4-year payback vs BAS 2-year payback. 3 Frazier Service Company – Cloud-based systems range 5,000-50,000; subscription models from $750/month. Understanding the CapEx Monitoring Model Traditional Building Management Systems represent the CapEx approach in this decision framework. These systems emerged in the 1980s as centralized platforms for controlling HVAC, lighting, fire safety, and security systems. A traditional building automation system provides direct automated control over building equipment through hardwired connections and proprietary protocols. This architecture enables complex automation sequences that can optimize comfort and energy use simultaneously, but requires substantial upfront capital investment. The typical CapEx monitoring installation involves extensive infrastructure investment. According to the NREL study, wired sensor installation costs 150-400 per point when accounting for conduit, wiring, and labor. On-premise servers require dedicated space, climate control, and IT maintenance. Annual maintenance contracts typically run 10-15% of the installed cost, with specialized technicians needed for programming and troubleshooting. For a 100,000 square foot facility, traditional building automation costs often exceed $500,000 before accounting for ongoing expenses. Despite these costs, CapEx monitoring remains appropriate in specific scenarios within the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring framework. New construction projects benefit from lower wiring costs during the building phase. Facilities requiring direct automated control over equipment without any delay benefit from local processing. Organizations with established facilities teams and technical expertise can maintain these systems effectively. Long-term building ownership spanning 15 or more years can amortize the capital investment across a timeline that makes the economics favorable. The NREL research also revealed that 50-75% of traditional BMS costs come from labor rather than hardware. This finding has significant implications for the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring decision, as it explains why retrofitting existing buildings with traditional systems costs substantially more than new construction installations. Labor-intensive wiring, programming, and commissioning drive costs upward in occupied facilities where work must be scheduled around tenants and operations. Understanding the OpEx Monitoring Model OpEx monitoring represents the modern subscription-based approach in the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring decision framework. Cloud-based platforms use wireless sensors that communicate through Wi-Fi, cellular, or LoRaWAN networks to transmit building data to centralized analytics engines. This architecture eliminates the need for on-premise servers, dedicated IT infrastructure, and extensive wiring. Wireless sensor installation costs 40-100 per point, representing 60-80% cost savings compared to wired alternatives. The primary advantage of OpEx monitoring is converting large capital expenses to predictable monthly operating costs. These systems excel at identifying patterns, detecting anomalies, and providing actionable insights that traditional systems often miss. Machine learning algorithms can predict equipment failures before they occur, detect energy waste in real-time, and benchmark performance across multiple facilities. Remote access through web and mobile applications enables facility managers to monitor operations from anywhere. Automatic software updates ensure the platform continuously improves without manual intervention or additional cost. OpEx vs CapEx monitoring deployment timelines differ dramatically. Subscription-based IoT monitoring deploys in days rather than months, which represents a fundamental shift in how facilities can approach building intelligence. Wireless sensors require no construction, minimal building disruption, and can be installed during normal business hours without displacing occupants or interrupting operations. The subscription model eliminates large upfront investments and simplifies budget approval processes that often delay traditional BMS projects for years. For organizations managing hotel portfolios or retail chains across multiple locations, OpEx monitoring provides consistent visibility that would be prohibitively expensive with traditional CapEx deployments. A single dashboard can display performance metrics across dozens or hundreds of properties, enabling portfolio-wide optimization strategies that identify best practices and underperforming outliers simultaneously.
Modern monitoring platforms provide comprehensive visibility across building systems, equipment, and environmental conditions. Key Differences in the OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring Decision Understanding the fundamental differences between these approaches is essential for making the right OpEx vs CapEx monitoring choice. Capital versus operating expense structures differ dramatically between these two approaches. Building management systems require large upfront capital investment with ongoing maintenance costs that appear in different budget categories. OpEx monitoring operates on subscription models that spread costs over time as operating expenses. This distinction matters significantly for budget planning and approval processes. Many organizations find operating expenses easier to approve since they do not require capital budget allocation, depreciation management, or multi-year financial commitments. Cash flow impact represents another critical distinction in the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring evaluation. CapEx approaches tie up significant capital that could be deployed elsewhere in the business. A $500,000 BMS investment represents opportunity cost beyond the direct expenditure. OpEx models preserve capital for core business activities while delivering monitoring capabilities through manageable monthly payments. For organizations with limited capital or competing investment priorities, this flexibility often determines which approach is practical. Deployment complexity varies substantially between these approaches and often determines which solution is practical for a given situation. Traditional building automation installation requires construction, wiring, programming, and commissioning phases that extend over 6-18 months. OpEx monitoring deployments typically complete in 5-10 days with minimal disruption. This difference affects time-to-value and the ability to respond quickly to emerging requirements. Organizations facing immediate compliance deadlines or rapid expansion needs often find OpEx timelines more practical than waiting over a year for traditional implementation. Scalability considerations favor OpEx architectures in the monitoring evaluation for organizations expecting growth or change. Adding sensors to a subscription platform requires only physical installation and network configuration that can be completed in hours. Expanding a traditional building management system often requires additional controller hardware, programming modifications, and potentially server upgrades that involve weeks of planning and implementation. For growing organizations or those managing geographically distributed portfolios, this flexibility significantly impacts long-term costs and operational simplicity. When to Choose the CapEx Monitoring Model The OpEx vs CapEx monitoring decision should favor capital investment when direct automated control is essential to operations and cannot be achieved through other means. Facilities requiring complex automation sequences that respond instantly to changing conditions benefit from local processing that eliminates network latency. Critical environments where any communication delay is unacceptable need the reliability of hardwired connections. Organizations with established technical teams capable of maintaining proprietary systems can leverage this expertise effectively to maximize system performance. Best Use Cases for CapEx Monitoring New Construction: Wiring costs are significantly lower when installed during building phase before walls are closed Critical Environments: Facilities requiring zero-latency automated control for safety or process requirements Long-Term Ownership: Buildings owned for 15+ years allowing capital costs to amortize favorably Technical Staff: Organizations with in-house facilities engineering expertise and capacity for ongoing system maintenance Organizations with capital budgets exceeding $500,000 allocated specifically for building automation should consider the CapEx approach when the use case requires direct control. When long-term ownership spanning 15 or more years is planned, the higher upfront cost can deliver favorable lifetime economics compared to ongoing subscription fees. Facilities requiring integration of fire safety, security, and building systems into a single platform with unified control logic may find traditional building management provides the necessary capabilities. When to Choose the OpEx Monitoring Model OpEx monitoring wins the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring comparison when visibility and analytics are the primary needs rather than direct equipment control. Organizations seeking to understand building performance, identify waste, and optimize operations benefit from the advanced analytics these platforms provide. The ability to deploy quickly addresses urgent requirements such as compliance deadlines, tenant complaints, or emerging energy costs that cannot wait for traditional implementation timelines. Facilities managing educational buildings with limited technical staff find managed services particularly valuable since the provider handles system maintenance and updates. Existing buildings present compelling economics for OpEx monitoring that often make traditional CapEx impractical. Retrofitting wired systems into occupied spaces costs 50-100% more than new construction installation due to the labor required to run conduit through finished walls and ceilings. The disruption of construction in occupied spaces affects tenant satisfaction and operations in ways that extend far beyond the direct project costs. Wireless sensors install without construction, during normal hours, with minimal impact on building occupants or ongoing operations. Capital-constrained organizations benefit significantly from the subscription model that OpEx vs CapEx monitoring enables. Converting large capital expenses to predictable monthly operating costs simplifies budgeting and approval while preserving capital for other priorities. Organizations without dedicated technical staff find managed services eliminate the need to hire specialists or train existing personnel on complex systems. Multi-building portfolios gain consistent visibility across all properties without replicating infrastructure investment at each location, creating economies of scale that CapEx approaches cannot match.
Monitoring dashboards provide real-time visibility into energy consumption and equipment performance across your facility. Common Mistakes in the OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring Decision Facility managers frequently make avoidable errors when evaluating OpEx vs CapEx monitoring options that lead to overspending, underperformance, or both. Understanding these common mistakes helps you avoid the pitfalls that derail building monitoring projects and leave organizations stuck with solutions that do not meet their actual needs. Mistake #1: Comparing Purchase Price Instead of Total Cost of Ownership Many organizations focus solely on upfront costs when the decision should consider 5-10 year total cost of ownership. Traditional systems have lower annual costs after installation but require significant capital upfront plus ongoing maintenance contracts. Subscription models have ongoing costs but minimal upfront investment. Calculate the full lifecycle cost including installation, maintenance, upgrades, staffing requirements, and eventual replacement before making your decision. Mistake #2: Overbuying Control Capabilities You Do Not Need Organizations often purchase traditional BMS for direct equipment control when visibility alone would solve their actual problems. If your primary goals are identifying energy waste, monitoring equipment performance, and receiving alerts about anomalies, OpEx monitoring delivers these capabilities at a fraction of the cost. Reserve CapEx investment for situations where automated control sequences genuinely improve operations beyond what visibility and manual adjustment can achieve. Mistake #3: Ignoring Implementation Timeline Impact The 6-18 month implementation timeline for traditional systems means you continue wasting energy and experiencing preventable problems throughout the deployment period. If your facility wastes $50,000 annually in energy, a 12-month implementation delay costs $50,000 in foregone savings before the system even comes online. OpEx vs CapEx monitoring deployed in 10 days begins delivering value almost immediately, often paying for itself before a traditional system would complete commissioning. Mistake #4: Assuming Existing Buildings Cannot Have Smart Technology Many facility managers believe their older buildings cannot benefit from modern monitoring because traditional retrofit costs are prohibitive. This assumption ignores the wireless alternative that installs without construction in any building regardless of age. The 90% of buildings without smart technology represent massive opportunities for OpEx monitoring that would never make economic sense with traditional wired systems. Real-World Example: Empire State Building Facility: Empire State Building Location: New York, NY Challenge: The 2.7 million square foot landmark building needed to modernize outdated control systems while demonstrating the business case for deep energy retrofits in historic properties. Solution: Empire State Realty Trust partnered with Johnson Controls to implement a comprehensive building management upgrade including digital controls, CO2 sensors, and advanced monitoring capabilities that replaced piecemeal pneumatic systems. Results: 38% guaranteed energy reduction with a 3-year payback. Exceeded targets every year since 2010, saving $4.4 million annually. Total savings of $858,305 from control system upgrades alone. The hybrid approach works particularly well for organizations evaluating OpEx vs CapEx monitoring options who want to proceed cautiously. You can start with subscription-based monitoring to establish baseline performance and identify opportunities, then make informed decisions about deeper automation investments based on actual data rather than projections. Many clients discover that visibility alone, without direct control, delivers 80% of the potential savings at 20% of the traditional building automation cost. Calculate your savings now -> Frequently Asked Questions About OpEx vs CapEx Monitoring What is the main difference between OpEx and CapEx monitoring? The fundamental difference in the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring comparison is how you pay for building intelligence. CapEx requires large upfront capital investment in hardware and software that you own and depreciate over time. OpEx converts monitoring into a subscription service with predictable monthly payments. The choice affects your balance sheet, cash flow, budget approval process, and total cost of ownership significantly over the system's lifetime. How much does traditional BMS installation cost compared to subscription monitoring? According to NREL research, traditional BMS installation costs 5.20-14.18 per square foot, while IoT monitoring costs approximately $0.75 per square foot – up to 5x less expensive upfront. For a 100,000 square foot facility, CapEx approaches typically require 520,000-1,418,000 upfront. OpEx approaches start from $750/month with minimal initial investment, resulting in 5-year TCO of 45,000-90,000. What is the typical ROI timeline for each approach? According to CREtech research, traditional BMS systems typically achieve payback in approximately 4 years, while OpEx vs CapEx monitoring with subscription models achieves payback in approximately 2 years. The faster OpEx ROI results from lower initial investment combined with rapid deployment that begins delivering savings within days rather than waiting 6-18 months for traditional installation to complete. How does technology obsolescence affect the OpEx vs CapEx monitoring decision? CapEx investments lock you into specific technology that may become outdated within 5-7 years. Upgrading to meet new compliance requirements or access new features often requires significant additional investment. OpEx subscription models include continuous updates and can incorporate hardware refresh, protecting against obsolescence and ensuring access to the latest capabilities without additional cost. Which approach is better for multi-facility portfolios? OpEx monitoring provides significant advantages for multi-building portfolios. Subscription costs scale predictably, centralized dashboards provide portfolio-wide visibility, and new facilities can be added quickly without capital budget constraints. CapEx approaches require separate capital investments for each location, often forcing organizations to phase deployments over multiple years due to budget limitations. What are the tax implications of each approach? CapEx investments depreciate over the asset's useful life (typically 5-7 years), spreading tax benefits over time. OpEx expenses are fully deductible in the year incurred, providing immediate tax benefits. Consult with your financial advisors to understand which approach provides more favorable treatment for your specific tax situation and cash flow requirements. How quickly can subscription monitoring be deployed? Most subscription-based IoT monitoring platforms deploy within 5-10 days from contract signing to full operation. Wireless sensors require minimal installation, cloud connectivity eliminates server requirements, and intuitive interfaces reduce training needs. Traditional BMS projects typically require 6-18 months for full deployment including procurement, installation, programming, integration, and commissioning phases. Can I combine OpEx monitoring with an existing BMS? Yes, OpEx monitoring can complement existing building automation installations effectively. The hybrid approach layers advanced analytics on top of legacy systems, providing insights that older platforms cannot deliver on their own. Organizations choosing the MaaS model can extend the value of existing investments while gaining cloud-based visibility and modern analytics capabilities without replacing functional infrastructure. Explore OpenCloud Events Press Releases In the News Resources Contact Us Back To Blog PUE, CUE and WUE: Sustainable Data Center Metrics Guide US Signal | August 02, 2023
Data centers are used to house and run IT operations worldwide. These facilities are typically large and require a lot of energy, and with sustainability concerns on the rise, data centers have been in the spotlight regarding their environmental impacts. As more and more businesses move to off-site IT sources, the need for a sustainable data center is essential for sustainability. Data centers use three efficiency metrics to measure sustainability — power usage effectiveness (PUE), carbon usage effectiveness (CUE) and water usage effectiveness (WUE). Learn more about these sustainability metrics and how data centers can use them to reduce environmental impact. Power Usage Effectiveness PUE is one of the most common data center utilization metrics. Data center PUE is the ratio of total data center energy use to IT equipment energy use. Total energy use refers to all of the facility's loads, including everything from the IT equipment to lighting and cooling systems. IT energy use refers to everything strictly related to IT computing, storage and networking equipment and the associated controls. To calculate a facility's PUE, you would divide the total energy usage by the IT equipment energy usage. PUE is typically calculated over time to determine a facility's overall performance vs. its baseline. When taking readings for the total calculation, take readings at the facility utility meter. If the data center is part of a larger facility, only take readings for the area dedicated to the data center. Then, measure the IT load with all power conversions, switching and conditioning on. Comparing PUE across multiple data centers is difficult because several factors can affect it, and each facility may use different configurations. Much of these differences depend on how a facility consumes its energy. For example, energy consumption can vary depending on factors like: Climate and weather Power grid services Building materials and layout PUE plays a significant role in data center sustainability because it provides facilities with important efficiency insights related to data center power consumption. Measuring PUE helps data centers achieve energy efficiency and operational cost savings. Carbon Usage Effectiveness CUE is a metric used to measure sustainability regarding data center carbon emissions. The metric is the ratio of total data center carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions to IT equipment energy consumption. You can calculate data center CUE by dividing the CO2 emissions from total energy use by IT equipment energy use. For the equation, carbon emissions can be based on local government data if the facility's electricity comes from the grid. If electricity is produced on-site, the readings can come from local meters. Where the energy comes from can affect how much a facility uses. CUE helps data centers understand how much of their total CO2 emissions are attributed to the energy IT equipment uses. With these insights, data centers can take action to reduce their environmental impact. Carbon emissions are also heavily regulated by various organizations, meaning data centers must comply with regulations regarding the production and handling of emissions. Measuring and tracking CUE helps facilities maintain compliance. Water Usage Effectiveness Data centers use water for cooling systems and generating energy. These facilities have a high demand for water, which isn't necessarily abundant, as water consumption continues to rise . As such, data center WUE measures the amount of water used to cool IT assets. Calculating a facility's WUE is the ratio of water used at the data center to the electricity delivered to IT hardware. Several factors affect a facility's WUE, including: Climate: Cooling is one of the primary uses of water in a data center. Depending on the climate where a facility is located, it may have a higher or lower need for cooling. Type of cooling system: The cooling system in the facility affects the WUE. Water cooling systems require a facility to use significantly more water than they would with a waterless cooling system. Waterless cooling systems can help data centers cut back on water usage. Power sources: Whether a facility generates power with water will also affect WUE. Depending on the power source used, water consumption varies. WUE plays a significant role in data center sustainability, helping facilities conserve water and optimize water management. On average, data centers consume several million gallons of water per day , which isn't sustainable over the long term. WUE allows data centers to quantify their water usage and determine where water consumption can be reduced. In order to lower water usage, data centers should switch to or be designed with waterless cooling systems or look for ways to use water more efficiently, like recycling water. The Synergy of PUE, CUE and WUE in Sustainable Data Center These three metrics are considered the triad of data center sustainability. Monitoring all three metrics gives facilities the most comprehensive view of their operations' efficiency, which helps in identifying operational progress. Improvement opportunities create the potential for cost savings, increased efficiency and overall sustainability. While these data center efficiency metrics should be used in conjunction, they don't necessarily consider each other in calculations. For example, using a water-based cooling system powered by electricity can improve PUE and worsen WUE. However, both metrics should be considered together to optimize efficiency. In this sense, facilities must balance trade-offs among the three metrics to achieve optimal sustainability. Together, PUE, CUE and WUE can impact data center sustainability in several ways, such as: Environmental benefits: Reduced power usage, carbon emissions and water consumption significantly reduce data centers' environmental impact. Cost savings: Data centers with sustainable business practices are eligible for government incentives and rebates, helping increase cost savings for the facility and its clients. Enhanced reputation for businesses: Businesses that partner with sustainable data centers gain a reputation for being eco-conscious, which bodes well with the majority of consumers. Partner With US Signal for Sustainable Data Center At US Signal, we're committed to operating our data centers in the most sustainable way possible. We're on the road to powering all our data centers with renewable energy, helping us reduce our carbon footprint and yours. Our green data centers are helping reduce our environmental impact while meeting your IT needs and getting you closer to your sustainability goals. Contact us with any questions or for additional information. Recent Posts: April 28, 2026 Infrastructure Isn't Location-Based Anymore. Here's What That Means. 2026-05-04 How to Check Monthly Server CPU Usage 2026-05-04 Can a Stardew Valley Server Run Multiple Modpacks? 2026-05-03 GTA 5 Online Stuck Loading: Is It Server-Side? 2026-05-02 How to Fix Long-Term High Server CPU Usage 2026-04-30 High latency in Dota 2 which fix works best 2026-04-29 Choose CPU cores easily for Japan servers 2026-04-28 Consumer vs Data Center GPU for AI Inference 2026-04-27 Knowledge-base PUE, WUE, CUE, IUE, TCO in Liquid-Cooled Data Centers Release Date: 2026-04-14 You need to track key metrics to run a liquid cooling data center efficiently. These key metrics help you see how well your cooling system works and show where you can save resources. For Hong Kong hosting environments, where energy costs and space are critical, tracking these metrics becomes even more important. Each metric tells you something different about your cooling operations. You can use the table below to see what each key metric covers and how it affects your goals: When you understand how to calculate and interpret these key metrics, you make better choices for both efficiency and sustainability. Paying attention to key metrics gives you a clearer picture of your cooling system's performance and helps you improve your data center's operations. Key Takeaways Track key metrics like PUE, WUE, and CUE to improve efficiency and sustainability in your data center. Lower PUE values indicate better energy efficiency, meaning more energy goes to IT tasks rather than cooling. Monitor WUE to reduce water usage and support sustainability goals, especially as water conservation becomes crucial. Use CUE to measure your carbon footprint and align your operations with environmental standards. Regularly review and adjust your metrics to optimize performance, reduce costs, and enhance resource management. Key Metrics for Liquid Cooling Data Centers Why Key Metrics Matter You need to measure the right data center metrics to understand how your liquid cooling system performs. These metrics act as key performance indicators for your facility. They help you see where you can improve efficiency, reduce costs, and boost sustainability. When you track metrics like pue, wue, and cue, you get a clear view of your energy use, water consumption, and carbon emissions. Real-time monitoring of these metrics lets you compare your cooling system to industry standards. You can spot problems early and make changes that keep your data center running smoothly. Operators use these numbers to guide decisions, set goals, and choose innovative it cooling solutions. As wue and cue become more important, you need to focus on more than just energy. You must also look at water and carbon impacts to meet new regulations and customer expectations. Overview of PUE, WUE, CUE, IUE, TCO You will find several main metrics that shape the way you manage a liquid cooling data center. Each one tells you something different about efficiency and sustainability. Here is a quick overview: You should use these metrics together to get a full picture of your data center's performance. By focusing on pue, wue, cue, iue, and tco, you can make smart choices that improve efficiency and support sustainability. Power Usage Effectiveness (PUE) and Energy Metrics What Is Power Usage Effectiveness (PUE)? You need to understand power usage effectiveness to measure how well your data center uses energy. Power usage effectiveness is a metric that compares the total energy used by your facility to the energy consumed by IT equipment. You calculate PUE using this formula: PUE = Total Facility Power ÷ IT Equipment Power This metric shows you how much of your energy consumption goes directly to computing tasks. When you see a PUE value close to 1.0, you know your data center uses energy efficiently. A PUE of 1.0 means all energy is used for IT equipment. Values above 1.0 show that some energy is lost to cooling and other non-IT functions. You can use power usage effectiveness to track energy efficiency metrics and improve your cooling performance. This metric helps you see how much energy is wasted and where you can make changes. You need to focus on lowering your PUE to boost efficiency and reduce energy consumption. Calculating and Interpreting PUE You can calculate power usage effectiveness by dividing the total energy used by your data center by the energy consumed by IT equipment. For example: If your data center uses 100,000 kW in total and 80,000 kW for IT equipment, you calculate PUE as follows: PUE = 100,000 kW / 80,000 kW = 1.25 This result shows that your facility uses more energy than just what is needed for computing, mostly for cooling and other support systems. You can compare your PUE to industry averages to see how your energy efficiency stacks up. The table below shows typical PUE ranges for different cooling methods: Liquid cooling systems help you achieve lower PUE values. They use less energy than air cooling systems and reduce dependence on energy-intensive air conditioning units. This means more of your energy consumption goes to computing tasks, not cooling. You can see the impact of cooling performance on PUE in recent case studies: You need to aim for a lower PUE to improve energy efficiency. Lower values mean your data center uses less energy for cooling and more for IT equipment. This leads to reduced operational costs and better data center energy usage. You can use power usage effectiveness to guide your energy efficiency metrics and make smart decisions about cooling performance. Tip: Track your PUE regularly. Use it to spot trends in energy consumption and cooling performance. This helps you find ways to optimize your total energy used and boost efficiency. You can see that power usage effectiveness is a key metric for measuring energy efficiency in liquid cooling data centers. It helps you understand energy consumption, improve cooling, and achieve better performance. Water and Carbon Usage Metrics: WUE and CUE Understanding Water Usage Effectiveness (WUE) You need to pay close attention to water usage effectiveness when you manage a sustainable data center. WUE measures how efficiently your facility uses water for cooling and IT operations. This metric helps you track water consumption and find ways to improve efficiency. Water usage effectiveness is calculated by dividing the total water consumed by your data center by the total energy used by IT equipment. The formula for WUE is: WUE = Total Water Consumed (liters) / Total Energy Consumed (kWh) For example, if your data center uses 100,000 liters of water and 50,000 kWh of energy, your WUE is 2.0 L/kWh. You can use WUE to compare different cooling methods and see which one supports better data center sustainability. The table below shows how WUE values differ between cooling systems: Liquid cooling systems often achieve a WUE near zero. This means you use very little water compared to other methods, which is important for data center sustainability. You can see that air cooling uses no water but has higher energy use, while evaporative cooling uses more water. By tracking WUE, you can make informed decisions that support both efficiency and environmental goals. Water efficiency is becoming more important as groundwater shortages increase. WUE helps you monitor and optimize water use, which directly impacts the sustainability of your operations. You should use WUE to: Track water usage in your facility. Identify areas where you can reduce water consumption. Support your sustainable data center goals. WUE is a key metric for data center sustainability. It helps you balance performance, efficiency, and environmental responsibility. Understanding Carbon Usage Effectiveness (CUE) You also need to focus on carbon usage effectiveness to measure your data center's environmental impact. CUE shows how much carbon dioxide your facility emits for every unit of energy used by IT equipment. This metric helps you understand and improve your carbon footprint. Carbon usage effectiveness is calculated by dividing total carbon emissions by IT equipment energy usage. The formula for CUE is: CUE = Total Carbon Emissions / IT Equipment Energy Usage You can also calculate CUE by multiplying your carbon emission factor by your PUE. CUE values can range from near zero in data centers powered by renewable energy to 0.3–0.7 kgCO₂/kWh in facilities that rely on fossil fuels. The ideal CUE is 0.0 kg CO₂ per kWh, which means your data center operates with maximum efficiency and minimal environmental impact. You should use CUE to: Measure the amount of carbon dioxide emitted per unit of energy consumed. Compare your facility's carbon efficiency to industry standards. Set goals for reducing your carbon footprint and improving data center sustainability. CUE plays a vital role in assessing the environmental effectiveness of your operations. Lower CUE values show that your data center is more efficient and supports global climate goals. Liquid cooling can help you achieve lower CUE by reducing energy use and supporting renewable energy integration. By tracking CUE, you can make your data center more sustainable and align with environmental regulations and customer expectations. Both WUE and CUE are essential metrics for any sustainable data center. They help you measure efficiency, manage resources, and reduce your environmental impact. When you use these metrics together, you gain a complete view of your facility's performance and sustainability. Infrastructure Usage and TCO Infrastructure Usage Effectiveness (IUE) You need to understand Infrastructure Usage Effectiveness to improve resource efficiency in your liquid cooling data center. IUE measures how well you use infrastructure resources, such as power, cooling, and space. This metric helps you find areas where you can boost efficiency and reduce waste. When you track IUE, you see how changes in cooling technology affect your facility. The introduction of liquid cooling leads to a 10.2% reduction in total data center power and over a 15% improvement in Total Usage Effectiveness. Server fan power consumption drops by 41% to 80%, which results in a 7% reduction in IT power. Liquid cooling allows you to use higher chilled water and secondary inlet temperatures. This change increases infrastructure efficiency and supports better resource allocation. You can use IUE to compare different cooling systems and see which one delivers the best efficiency. When you focus on this metric, you make smarter decisions about upgrades and investments. Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) and Metric Integration You need to look at total cost of ownership when you plan your data center's future. TCO includes all costs over the life of your cooling system. This metric covers initial setup, ongoing maintenance, energy use, and downtime. You can use TCO to guide financial planning and make sure your investments support long-term efficiency. Initial costs include CDUs, manifolds, connectors, and cold plates. Lifetime electricity costs cover servers, CDUs, and heat rejection mechanisms. Installation costs involve plumbing, leak testing, and commissioning. Ongoing maintenance includes CDUs, coolant, connectors, and filters. You must also consider costs related to downtime and software updates, ease of commissioning, and expected cooling system uptime. When you integrate metrics like IUE, PUE, WUE, and CUE into your TCO analysis, you get a complete view of your data center's efficiency and sustainability. You can use these metrics to compare energy, maintenance, capital expenses, and water costs. This approach helps you optimize performance and control expenses. Tip: Track all metrics regularly. Use them to spot trends and make informed decisions about cooling upgrades and resource allocation. Comparing and Optimizing Key Metrics Balancing Efficiency, Sustainability, and Cost You need to understand how metrics interact when you optimize a liquid cooling data center. Efficiency, sustainability, and cost often compete with each other. Liquid cooling systems improve energy efficiency and lower operational costs compared to air cooling. You see lifecycle advantages, such as reduced mechanical complexity and better thermal stability. These benefits support sustainability by minimizing water usage and emissions. However, you face a higher initial investment. For example, the upfront cost difference can reach €739,000, but annual savings of €380,000 lead to a total cost reduction of €1.16 million over five years. You can amortize the initial expense quickly while reducing carbon emissions. Liquid cooling also offers enhanced thermal management and scalability. You meet the demands of modern data centers and address sustainability concerns. When you optimize for efficiency, you may need to balance the impact on other metrics. Lowering PUE can increase capital costs, while reducing WUE supports sustainability but may affect cooling performance. You must evaluate each metric and consider trade-offs to achieve the best overall optimization. Tip: Review your metrics regularly. Use real-world data to guide decisions and adjust your strategy as your data center evolves. Strategies for Metric Optimization You can use several strategies to optimize metrics in liquid cooling data centers. These approaches help you improve efficiency, sustainability, and cost control. Install liquid cooling systems to achieve lower PUE values, typically between 1.1 and 1.2. Use automated demand response and real-time monitoring through energy management systems to boost cooling efficiency and reduce operational costs. Design your data center with scalability in mind. Enhanced thermal management supports both performance and sustainability. Validate your optimization models with operational sensor data. Digital twin frameworks, like those used at Oak Ridge National Laboratory, help you measure and control metrics accurately. Track space utilization and cabinet count. Liquid cooling can reduce cabinet numbers by up to 67%, improving infrastructure efficiency. Focus on heuristic-based optimization and real-time measurement. These trends help you adapt quickly and maintain high efficiency. The table below shows how metric optimization impacts real-world projects: You need to combine these strategies to optimize all metrics. By focusing on efficiency, sustainability, and cost, you create a high-performance data center that meets future demands. You gain a clear advantage by tracking key metrics in your liquid cooling data center. Regular monitoring of cooling systems helps you reduce power usage, water consumption, and carbon emissions. You also improve performance and qualify for cost-saving incentives. To keep your facility efficient, you should: Use real-time monitoring tools for all metrics. Benchmark against industry standards. Adjust cooling operations as workloads change. Support sustainability goals with transparent reporting. Stay committed to ongoing monitoring and improvement. These metrics guide you to smarter decisions for efficiency, sustainability, and cost control. FAQ What is the main benefit of liquid cooling in data centers? You gain higher efficiency with liquid cooling. This method removes heat faster than air systems. You also reduce energy use and improve performance. Many operators choose liquid cooling to support high-density servers. How does liquid cooling affect sustainability? Liquid cooling uses less water and energy. You lower your carbon footprint and support green goals. This method helps you meet environmental standards and conserve resources. Can you monitor cooling metrics in real time? You can track cooling metrics with sensors and software. Real-time monitoring lets you spot issues quickly. You adjust operations to maintain optimal performance and prevent downtime. Which metric should you focus on first? You should start with PUE. This metric shows how efficiently you use energy. Lower PUE values mean your cooling system works better and supports cost savings. Does liquid cooling increase upfront costs? Liquid cooling often costs more at first. You save money over time with lower energy bills and maintenance. Many data centers see a quick return on investment. Source: Root Cause Analysis Explained: Definition, Examples, and Methods - Tableau
URL: https://www.tableau.com/analytics/what-is-root-cause-analysis
Root Cause Analysis Explained: Definition, Examples, and Methods | Tableau Skip to content Menu Products Toggle sub-navigation for Products Overview Tableau Next Toggle sub-navigation for Tableau Next Tableau Semantics Tableau Cloud Tableau Server Tableau Desktop Latest Release Customers Toggle sub-navigation for Customers Customer Stories Community Stories Solutions Toggle sub-navigation for Solutions By Industry Toggle sub-navigation for By Industry Financial Services Healthcare and Life Sciences Public Sector Retail and Consumer Goods Communications and Media Manufacturing By Role Toggle sub-navigation for By Role Business Leader Data and IT Leader Analyst Developer Marketing Finance Sales Support and Service By Technology Tableau Exchange Toggle sub-navigation for Tableau Exchange Accelerators Dashboard Showcase Resources Toggle sub-navigation for Resources Getting Started Learn Tableau Toggle sub-navigation for Learn Tableau Webinars Whitepapers Articles Free Training Videos Build Data Literacy Instructor-led Training Tableau eLearning Academic Programs Community Blog Events Support Tableau Help Trailhead Pricing Toggle sub-navigation for Pricing Pricing Calculator Buy Tableau+ Contact us 1-800-270-6977 Sign in Create account Start for free Buy now Free training Partner portal Get Tableau for free Contact sales Start for free Cancel Search Root Cause Analysis Explained: Definition, Examples, and Methods Try Tableau for Free The easiest way to understand root cause analysis is to think about common problems. If we're sick and throwing up at work, we'll go to a doctor and ask them to find the root cause of our sickness. If our car stops working, we'll ask a mechanic to find the root cause of the problem. If our business is underperforming (or overperforming) in a certain area, we'll try to find out why. For each of these examples, we could just find a simple remedy for each symptom. To stop throwing up at work, we might stay home with a bucket. To get around without a car, we might take the bus and leave our broken car at home. But these solutions only consider the symptoms and do not consider the underlying causes of those symptoms—causes like a stomach infection that requires medicine or a busted car alternator that needs to be repaired. To solve or analyze a problem, we'll need to perform a root cause analysis and find out exactly what the cause is and how to fix it. In this article, we'll cover the following: Definition of root cause analysis Benefits and goals of root cause analysis How to conduct root cause analysis Tips for performing rot cause analysis What is root cause analysis? Root cause analysis (RCA) is the process of discovering the root causes of problems in order to identify appropriate solutions. RCA assumes that it is much more effective to systematically prevent and solve for underlying issues rather than just treating ad hoc symptoms and putting out fires. Root cause analysis can be performed with a collection of principles, techniques, and methodologies that can all be leveraged to identify the root causes of an event or trend. Looking beyond superficial cause and effect, RCA can show where processes or systems failed or caused an issue in the first place. Core principles There are a few core principles that guide effective root cause analysis, some of which should already be apparent. Not only will these help the analysis quality, these will also help the analyst gain trust and buy-in from stakeholders, clients, or patients. Focus on correcting and remedying root causes rather than just symptoms. Don't ignore the importance of treating symptoms for short term relief. Realize there can be, and often are, multiple root causes. Focus on HOW and WHY something happened, not WHO was responsible. Be methodical and find concrete cause-effect evidence to back up root cause claims. Provide enough information to inform a corrective course of action. Consider how a root cause can be prevented (or replicated) in the future. As the above principles illustrate: when we analyze deep issues and causes, it's important to take a comprehensive and holistic approach. In addition to discovering the root cause, we should strive to provide context and information that will result in an action or a decision. Remember: good analysis is actionable analysis. Benefits and goals of root cause analysis The first goal of root cause analysis is to discover the root cause of a problem or event. The second goal is to fully understand how to fix, compensate, or learn from any underlying issues within the root cause. The third goal is to apply what we learn from this analysis to systematically prevent future issues or to repeat successes. Analysis is only as good as what we do with that analysis, so the third goal of RCA is important. We can use RCA to also modify core process and system issues in a way that prevents future problems. Instead of just treating the symptoms of a football player's concussion, for example, root cause analysis might suggest wearing a helmet to reduce the risk of future concussions. Treating the individual symptoms may feel productive. Solving a large number of problems looks like something is getting done. But if we don't actually diagnose the real root cause of a problem we'll likely have the same exact problem over and over. Instead of a news editor just fixing every single omitted Oxford comma, she will prevent further issues by training her writers to use commas properly in all future assignments. Create beautiful visualizations with your data. Try Tableau for free How to conduct an effective root cause analysis: techniques and methods There are a large number of techniques and strategies that we can use for root cause analysis, and this is by no means an exhaustive list. Below we'll cover some of the most common and most widely useful techniques. 5 Whys One of the more common techniques in performing a root cause analysis is the 5 Whys approach . We may also think of this as the annoying toddler approach. For every answer to a WHY question, follow it up with an additional, deeper “Ok, but WHY?” question. Children are surprisingly effective at root cause analysis. Common wisdom suggests that about five WHY questions can lead us to most root causes—but we could need as few as two or as many as 50 WHYs. Example: Let's think back to our football concussion example. First, our player will present a problem: Why do I have such a bad headache? This is our first WHY. First answer: Because I can't see straight. Second why: Why can't you see straight? Second answer: Because I my head hit the ground. Third why: Why did your head hit the ground? Third answer: I got hit tackled to the ground and hit my head hard. Fourth why: Why did hitting the ground hurt so much? Fourth answer: Because I wasn't wearing a helmet. Fifth why: Why weren't you wearing a helmet? Fifth answer: Because we didn't have enough helmets in our locker room. Aha. After these five questions, we discover that the root cause of the concussion was most likely from a lack of available helmets. In the future, we could reduce the risk of this type of concussion by making sure every football player has a helmet. The 5 Whys serve as a way to avoid assumptions. By finding detailed responses to incremental questions, answers become clearer and more concise each time. Ideally, the last WHY will lead to a process that failed, one which can then be fixed. Change Analysis/Event Analysis Another useful method of exploring root cause analysis is to carefully analyze the changes leading up to an event. This method is especially handy when there are a large number of potential causes. Instead of looking at the specific day or hour that something went wrong, we look at a longer period of time and gain a historical context.
First, we'd list out every potential cause leading up to an event. These should be any time a change occurred for better or worse or benign. Example: Let's say the event we're going to analyze is an uncharacteristically successful day of sales in New York City, and we wanted to know why it was so great so we can try to replicate it. First, we'd list out every touch point with each of the major customers, every event, every possibly relevant change.
Second, we'd categorize each change or event by how much influence we had over it. We can categorize as Internal/External, Owned/Unowned, or something similar. Example: In our great Sales day example, we'd start to sort out things like “Sales representative presented new slide deck on social impact” (Internal) and other events like “Last day of the quarter” (External) or “First day of Spring” (External).
Third, we'd go event by event and decide whether or not that event was an unrelated factor, a correlated factor, a contributing factor, or a likely root cause. This is where the bulk of the analysis happens and this is where other techniques like the 5 Whys can be used. Example: Within our analysis we discover that our fancy new Sales slide deck was actually an unrelated factor but the fact it was the end of the quarter was definitely a contributing factor. However, one factor was identified as the most likely root cause: the Sales Lead for the area moved to a new apartment with a shorter commute, meaning that she started showing up to meetings with clients 10 minutes earlier during the last week of the quarter.
Fourth, we look to see how we can replicate or remedy the root cause. Example: While not everyone can move to a new apartment, our organization decides that if Sales reps show up an extra 10 minutes earlier to client meetings in the final week of a quarter, they may be able to replicate this root cause success. Cause and effect Fishbone diagram Another common technique is creating a Fishbone diagram, also called an Ishikawa diagram , to visually map cause and effect. This can help identify possible causes for a problem by encouraging us to follow categorical branched paths to potential causes until we end up at the right one. It's similar to the 5 Whys but much more visual. Typically we start with the problem in the middle of the diagram (the spine of the fish skeleton), then brainstorm several categories of causes, which are then placed in off-shooting branches from the main line (the rib bones of the fish skeleton). Categories are very broad and might include things like “People” or “Environment.” After grouping the categories, we break those down into the smaller parts. For example, under “People” we might consider potential root cause factors like “leadership,” “staffing,” or “training.” As we dig deeper into potential causes and sub-causes, questioning each branch, we get closer to the sources of the issue. We can use this method to eliminate unrelated categories and identify correlated factors and likely root causes. For the sake of simplicity, carefully consider the categories before creating a diagram. Common categories to consider in a Fishbone diagram: Machine (equipment, technology) Method (process) Material (includes raw material, consumables, and information) Man/mind power (physical or knowledge work) Measurement (inspection) Mission (purpose, expectation) Management / money power (leadership) Maintenance Product (or service) Price Promotion (marketing) Process (systems) People (personnel) Physical evidence Performance Surroundings (place, environment) Suppliers Skills Tips for performing effective root cause analysis Ask questions to clarify information and bring us closer to answers. The more we can drill down and interrogate every potential cause, the more likely we are to find a root cause. Once we believe we have identified the root cause of the problem (and not just another symptom), we can ask even more questions: Why are we certain this is the root cause instead of that? How can we fix this root cause to prevent the issue from happening again? Use simple questions like “why?” “how?” and “so what does that mean here?” to carve a path towards understanding. Work with a team and get fresh eyes Whether it's just a partner or a whole team of colleagues, any extra eyes will help us figure out solutions faster and also serve as a check against bias. Getting input from others will also offer additional points of view, helping us to challenge our assumptions. Plan for future root cause analysis As we perform a root cause analysis, it's important to be aware of the process itself. Take notes. Ask questions about the analysis process itself. Find out if a certain technique or method works best for your specific business needs and environments. Remember to perform root cause analysis for successes too Root cause analysis is a great tool for figuring out where something went wrong. We typically use RCA as a way to diagnose problems but it can be equally as effective to find the root cause of a success. If we find the cause of a success or overachievement or early deadline, it's rarely a bad idea to find out the root cause of why things are going well. This kind of analysis can help prioritize and preemptively protect key factors and we might be able to translate success in one area of business to success in another area. Connect with your customers and boost your bottom line with actionable insights. Try Tableau for free Buy Tableau now Try for free What is Tableau?
